# T2 — Lever Architecture Standard

## Document Status
**System:** Market-Intelligence Operating System  
**Layer:** Intelligence Layer  
**Designation:** T2  
**Document Type:** Standard  
**Status:** Canonical Draft  
**Purpose Class:** Influence Mapping / Activation Logic / System Leverage Analysis

---

## 1. Purpose

The **Lever Architecture** is the authoritative influence-and-activation artifact for the Market-Intelligence Operating System.

Its function is to identify the mechanisms through which movement actually occurs inside an ecosystem: what creates traction, what accelerates adoption, what amplifies trust, what shifts behavior, what converts attention into action, and what bottlenecks or deadens momentum.

This document exists because ecosystems are not governed only by formal structure. They are governed by leverage. Some actors, signals, channels, incentives, rituals, moments, and interfaces have disproportionate power to change outcomes. T2 exists to reveal that architecture.

Its role is to answer not merely **who exists in the ecosystem**, but **what actually moves the ecosystem**.

---

## 2. Strategic Role in the Operating System

The Ecosystem Analysis identifies the terrain. T1 reveals the cultural meaning structure. T2 explains the operating mechanics of movement inside that terrain.

T2 serves nine strategic roles:

1. **Influence detection** — identifies who or what exerts outsized downstream effects.
2. **Adoption logic mapping** — explains how interest becomes trial, trial becomes habit, and habit becomes spread.
3. **Bottleneck identification** — reveals where momentum stalls.
4. **Signal hierarchy clarification** — distinguishes what gets ignored from what gets acted upon.
5. **Channel relevance mapping** — identifies where leverage actually lives versus where visibility merely appears to live.
6. **Trigger recognition** — surfaces moments or conditions that make behavior more likely to shift.
7. **System asymmetry exposure** — reveals where small interventions may have large effects.
8. **Strategic force selection** — helps later strategy choose entry points that work with the ecosystem’s motion rather than against it.
9. **Validation grounding** — gives T15 and later work a more realistic basis for deciding what to test and where.

T2 is therefore the operating system’s leverage-intelligence layer.

---

## 3. What This Document Is

This document is:

- a map of ecosystem leverage mechanics
- an influence architecture analysis
- a signal, trigger, and bottleneck document
- a downstream input to strategy, trust, timing, and entry work
- a mechanism-level complement to participant and cultural analysis

---

## 4. What This Document Is Not

The Lever Architecture is **not**:

- a generic funnel diagram
- a marketing channel plan
- an org chart of ecosystem actors
- a feature roadmap
- a list of influencers without force analysis
- a growth-playbook substitute

It should not confuse visibility with leverage, or activity with movement.

---

## 5. When to Commission or Use It

T2 should be commissioned once the Ecosystem Analysis has defined the participant landscape and once there is a need to understand how adoption, behavior, trust, and momentum actually propagate.

It becomes mandatory whenever any of the following are true:

- later strategic work depends on choosing an entry point or wedge
- influence, coordination, or trust transfer seem material to success
- there is uncertainty about what actually causes movement in the ecosystem
- T10 Entry Vector Analysis will be produced
- messaging, channel, partnership, or timing decisions risk being made on false assumptions about leverage

T2 is most useful when the ecosystem appears dynamic, networked, socially mediated, or bottlenecked by specific triggers or gatekeepers.

---

## 6. Mandatory vs Conditional Use

**Mandatory** for:

- ecosystems where adoption, trust, and action are socially or platform mediated
- ecosystems with visible gatekeepers, amplifiers, influencers, or chokepoints
- any work that will later produce T10 Entry Vector Analysis
- any opportunity where channel choice, partner choice, or timing could materially alter outcomes

**Conditionally recommended** for:

- highly simple or linear transactional ecosystems
- small markets where leverage is weakly distributed or trivially understood
- early scans before force selection becomes decision-relevant

Where disproportionate movement exists, T2 should be treated as required.

---

## 7. Inputs Required

The Lever Architecture requires the following inputs:

### 7.1 Foundational Inputs
- Ecosystem Analysis Section 1 participant map
- Ecosystem Analysis Section 3 social levers and influence structures
- Ecosystem Analysis Section 4 interface or infrastructure friction where relevant

### 7.2 Intelligence Inputs
- T1 Culture Map where already available
- observed community behaviors and adoption patterns
- examples of how ideas, tools, or offers have spread or failed to spread inside the ecosystem

### 7.3 Evidence Inputs
- native conversations and interaction patterns
- creator and operator content
- case examples of ecosystem growth or stagnation
- platform dynamics where relevant
- operational anecdotes where supported by stronger evidence or repeated patterns

### 7.4 Governance Inputs
- source quality requirements from the base standard
- assumption visibility where leverage is inferred rather than directly observable

---

## 8. Outputs Produced

T2 produces the following outputs:

- the dominant levers in the ecosystem
- the mechanisms through which those levers exert force
- bottlenecks and choke points
- trigger conditions that increase the probability of movement
- signal hierarchies and amplifier systems
- maps of where leverage is concentrated and where it is diffused
- an initial assessment of where intervention could produce disproportionate effect

These outputs feed directly into T3, T5, T6, T7, T10, and later concept and validation work.

---

## 9. Position in Sequencing Logic

T2 belongs near the front of the Intelligence layer.

Recommended sequence:

1. Ecosystem Analysis completed
2. T1 Culture Map produced or underway
3. **T2 Lever Architecture produced**
4. T3 Participant Decision Architecture can refine individual-level movement logic
5. T5 Competitive Topology can interpret competitive position with leverage context
6. T10 Entry Vector Analysis can later synthesize where leverage is most usable

T2 can be produced in parallel with T1, but it becomes stronger when cultural status, ritual, and trust patterns from T1 are available.

---

## 10. Relationship to Other Documents

### Upstream Relationships
T2 depends primarily on:

- **Ecosystem Analysis §1** for participant and role context
- **Ecosystem Analysis §3** for preliminary influence and leverage signals
- **Ecosystem Analysis §4** where infrastructure friction shapes movement
- **T1 Culture Map** where culture determines who or what gets amplified

### Downstream Relationships
T2 directly supports:

- **T3 Participant Decision Architecture** by clarifying which external levers shape participant choices
- **T5 Competitive Topology** by revealing where advantage is concentrated structurally
- **T6 Trust Architecture** by showing how trust propagates or stalls
- **T7 Temporal Rhythm Map** by identifying trigger moments and leverage windows
- **T10 Entry Vector Analysis** by revealing where intervention could produce outsized effect
- later messaging, partnership, distribution, or validation design work after the stack is complete

---

## 11. Core Analytical Questions

T2 should allow a reviewer to answer the following questions quickly:

1. What actually moves this ecosystem?
2. Which actors, signals, or conditions have disproportionate influence?
3. What channels amplify action rather than merely create exposure?
4. What triggers cause participants to pay attention, adopt, or switch behavior?
5. Where does movement stall?
6. Which bottlenecks or gatekeepers control flow?
7. What kinds of interventions are likely to have leverage versus low impact?
8. Where is leverage centralized, and where is it distributed?
9. How do culture, status, and incentives alter leverage?
10. What would make an entrant’s efforts compound rather than dissipate?

---

## 12. Required Structure of the Document

Every Lever Architecture must contain the following eight sections.

### 12.1 Section 1 — Dominant Levers
**Governing question:** What are the main mechanisms that create meaningful movement in this ecosystem?

This section must identify 5–10 dominant levers.

For each lever, document:

- what it is
- what it influences
- how strong it appears to be
- whether it is direct, indirect, symbolic, technical, social, or economic

### 12.2 Section 2 — Influence Pathways
**Governing question:** Through what pathways do levers exert force?

This section must map:

- who influences whom
- through what medium or interface
- with what delay, decay, or distortion
- where influence converts into action and where it only produces awareness

### 12.3 Section 3 — Trigger Conditions
**Governing question:** Under what conditions are participants most likely to move?

This section must identify the moments, events, states, or pressures that cause decision thresholds to shift.

Examples may include:

- seasonal changes
- platform changes
- social proof accumulation
- fear, urgency, prestige, cost shocks, or status opportunities

### 12.4 Section 4 — Bottlenecks and Choke Points
**Governing question:** Where does movement stall or get filtered?

This section must identify:

- adoption bottlenecks
- trust bottlenecks
- access bottlenecks
- technical bottlenecks
- approval bottlenecks
- attention bottlenecks

### 12.5 Section 5 — Amplifiers and Dampeners
**Governing question:** What magnifies movement, and what suppresses it?

This section must distinguish between factors that:

- increase spread, trust, or action
- reduce spread, trust, or action
- create asymmetry between actors or messages

### 12.6 Section 6 — Signal Hierarchy
**Governing question:** Which signals matter most inside the ecosystem, and which are treated as weak or ignorable?

This section must identify:

- high-trust signals
- low-trust signals
- signals that matter only in certain sub-tribes or contexts
- how signal meaning changes with source or channel

### 12.7 Section 7 — Leverage Concentration Map
**Governing question:** Where is leverage concentrated, and where is it diffuse?

This section must assess:

- whether the ecosystem is gatekeeper-driven or decentralized
- where a new entrant would need to plug in to matter
- whether leverage is owned, borrowed, rented, or emergent

### 12.8 Section 8 — Intervention Implications
**Governing question:** What kinds of moves appear most likely to create outsized effects?

This section must remain analytical, not prescriptive product planning.

It should identify:

- which leverage zones look promising
- which intervention types appear likely to compound
- which kinds of moves appear likely to fail because they ignore ecosystem mechanics

---

## 13. Required Data Fields

At minimum, every T2 document should include the following fields.

### 13.1 Header Fields
- **Document ID**
- **Ecosystem name**
- **Version**
- **Date**
- **Prepared by**
- **Source Ecosystem Analysis ID**
- **Status**

### 13.2 Lever Fields
- **Lever name**
- **Lever type**
- **What it influences**
- **Strength assessment**
- **Primary evidence sources**

### 13.3 Influence Pathway Fields
- **Source actor / signal**
- **Target actor / behavior**
- **Medium / channel**
- **Delay / decay note**
- **Action conversion note**

### 13.4 Trigger Fields
- **Trigger condition**
- **Why it matters**
- **Affected participants**
- **Observed consequence**

### 13.5 Bottleneck Fields
- **Bottleneck type**
- **Location in ecosystem**
- **What it blocks**
- **Severity**

### 13.6 Amplifier / Dampener Fields
- **Factor name**
- **Amplifier / dampener classification**
- **Mechanism of effect**
- **Scope of impact**

### 13.7 Signal Fields
- **Signal**
- **Signal source**
- **Trust level**
- **Context dependence**
- **Interpretation note**

### 13.8 Concentration Fields
- **Leverage zone**
- **Concentration level**
- **Owner / controller**
- **Implication for entrants**

### 13.9 Intervention Fields
- **Intervention zone**
- **Why leverage appears high**
- **What failure mode to avoid**

---

## 14. Dominant Levers Standard

This section must identify the few forces that actually move the ecosystem rather than listing every plausible influence.

Recommended lever categories include:

- social influence
- status transfer
- technical interoperability
- cost pressure
- urgency or deadline pressure
- policy or platform change
- trust endorsement
- habit formation
- workflow dependency
- visibility amplification

The section should prioritize explanatory power over completeness.

---

## 15. Influence Pathways Standard

This section must map movement pathways rather than static importance.

For each major pathway, the analysis should clarify:

- where force originates
- how it travels
- where it weakens
- where it becomes consequential action
- which actors can translate influence across boundaries

The goal is to identify the ecosystem’s motion mechanics, not just its loudest voices.

---

## 16. Trigger Conditions Standard

Triggers are the threshold shifters of the ecosystem.

This section should identify not only recurring moments, but also the emotional, financial, reputational, or technical states that make participants more likely to act.

Where possible, distinguish:

- chronic conditions that keep participants primed
- acute events that provoke action
- symbolic moments that heighten responsiveness

---

## 17. Bottlenecks and Choke Points Standard

Bottlenecks are leverage in reverse.

This section should identify where otherwise promising movement fails because:

- approval is required
- trust is insufficient
- switching cost is too high
- technical integration is too difficult
- information does not convert into action
- the wrong actor controls the next step

This section is critical because many weak strategies fail not from bad ideas but from ignored chokepoints.

---

## 18. Amplifiers and Dampeners Standard

This section must identify what makes a signal or intervention more potent or less potent than expected.

Amplifiers may include:

- status endorsement
- public visibility
- urgency compression
- social proof
- compatibility with existing rituals or habits

Dampeners may include:

- skepticism
- fragmented demand
- trust deficits
- complexity friction
- weak channel-message fit

---

## 19. Signal Hierarchy Standard

Not all signals are equal.

This section must distinguish between:

- signals that create belief
- signals that merely create awareness
- signals that matter only when emitted by certain actors
- signals that are discounted because they are overused, self-interested, or culturally foreign

Where possible, relate signal meaning back to T1 cultural hierarchy and taboo structure.

---

## 20. Leverage Concentration Map Standard

This section must identify whether leverage is:

- centralized in a few actors or interfaces
- decentralized but patterned
- highly fragmented
- platform-controlled
- community-owned
- transient and event-driven

This matters because entry tactics differ radically depending on whether leverage is concentrated or diffuse.

---

## 21. Intervention Implications Standard

This section must stay analytical.

It should identify:

- where leverage appears most usable
- which intervention classes appear promising
- which parts of the ecosystem are likely too inert or too defended
- where a small move might create disproportionate downstream effect

It should not propose product features, campaigns, or implementation plans directly.

---

## 22. Research Methodology Standard

The Lever Architecture should follow a mechanism-detection methodology.

Recommended minimum protocol:

### 22.1 Movement Case Review
Examine examples of adoption, spread, coordination, or trust transfer inside the ecosystem.

### 22.2 Failure Case Review
Examine examples where promising ideas, tools, or messages failed to move the ecosystem and identify why.

### 22.3 Actor-to-Actor Influence Mapping
Review how high-trust actors affect adjacent participants and where those effects break down.

### 22.4 Trigger Observation
Look for the conditions that repeatedly precede visible changes in behavior or attention.

The methodology should prioritize observed movement over abstract causal speculation.

---

## 23. Source and Evidence Expectations

The Lever Architecture must remain evidence-based even when some movement logic is partly inferential.

Source expectations:

- rely first on observed ecosystem behavior, repeat patterns, and primary ecosystem material where feasible
- use multiple examples before elevating a mechanism into a “dominant lever” when possible
- flag leverage claims as inferential where direct observation is thin
- distinguish durable levers from temporary anomalies

The document should be comfortable with structured inference, but not with unsupported causal storytelling.

---

## 24. Length Calibration

Recommended target length: **3,500–5,500 words**.  
Maximum: **7,000 words**.

If the document materially exceeds the maximum, the analysis may be cataloguing too many low-power mechanisms instead of isolating the dominant leverage architecture.

---

## 25. Required Usage Rules

The following rules are mandatory.

### Rule 1 — No visibility-for-leverage confusion
High visibility does not automatically equal high ecosystem leverage.

### Rule 2 — No generic funnel substitution
T2 must reflect ecosystem-specific movement mechanics, not imported marketing abstractions.

### Rule 3 — No bottleneck omission
Choke points and dampeners must be treated as integral to leverage analysis.

### Rule 4 — No static actor lists
Actors must be analyzed in terms of what force they exert and through what pathway.

### Rule 5 — No product contamination
T2 may not contain feature plans or build proposals.

### Rule 6 — No signal flattening
Different signals, sources, and channels must be distinguished by trust and consequence.

### Rule 7 — No overcounting minor mechanisms
The document should isolate dominant levers, not create an exhaustive list of every possible influence.

---

## 26. Recommended Operating Procedure

### Step 1 — Anchor in the Ecosystem Analysis
Use the participant map and preliminary leverage signals as the foundation.

### Step 2 — Identify movement cases
Study how ideas, tools, or behaviors have actually spread or stalled in the ecosystem.

### Step 3 — Isolate dominant levers
Determine which forces consistently explain movement.

### Step 4 — Map pathways, triggers, and bottlenecks
Clarify how leverage travels, when it activates, and where it dies.

### Step 5 — Differentiate amplifiers, dampeners, and signal hierarchies
Explain why some moves compound and others disappear.

### Step 6 — Assess leverage concentration
Determine whether entry requires centralized access, distributed trust, or hybrid motion.

### Step 7 — Produce intervention implications
Translate the mechanism map into analytical conclusions that later strategy can use.

---

## 27. Quality Standard / Acceptance Criteria

The Lever Architecture should be considered acceptable only if all of the following are true:

1. The dominant levers are clearly identified.
2. Influence pathways are mapped rather than implied.
3. Trigger conditions are specific and credible.
4. Bottlenecks and choke points are explicit.
5. Amplifiers and dampeners are distinguished clearly.
6. Signal hierarchy is mapped with attention to trust and source context.
7. Leverage concentration is assessed clearly.
8. Intervention implications remain analytical rather than drifting into product planning.
9. Evidence and inference are distinguished where needed.
10. The document gives later strategic work a realistic picture of how movement happens inside the ecosystem.

---

## 28. Failure Modes and Misuse Warnings

Common failure modes include:

### 28.1 Channel fetishism
Mistaking a visible channel for a true lever without showing how action actually propagates.

### 28.2 Actor glorification
Assuming famous actors matter most when structural or ritual levers may matter more.

### 28.3 Trigger vagueness
Using broad terms like “social proof” without specifying what kind, from whom, and under what conditions.

### 28.4 Bottleneck blindness
Ignoring the point where leverage fails to convert into adoption or trust.

### 28.5 Overmechanization
Treating the ecosystem like a machine while ignoring cultural or symbolic mediation already surfaced in T1.

### 28.6 Intervention drift
Allowing the document to become a tactics memo rather than a mechanism analysis.

### 28.7 Evidence thinness
Making strong causal claims from single anecdotes or weakly repeated patterns.

---

## 29. Minimum Template

The following structure should be included in implementation.

## 29.1 Lever Architecture Template

### Header
- Document ID
- Ecosystem Name
- Version
- Date
- Prepared By

- Status

### 1. Dominant Levers

|---|---|---|---|---|

|---|---|---|---|

- T1 if available

- case examples of spread, stall, or trust transfer

---

## 30. Relationship to Agentic Research Systems

The Lever Architecture should be structured so that agents can help surface recurring movement patterns without mistaking extraction for explanation.

This means:

- fields should separate levers, pathways, triggers, bottlenecks, and signals clearly
- evidence provenance should remain explicit
- inferred mechanisms should be distinguishable from directly observed ones
- the final leverage interpretation should remain reviewable rather than fully automated

An agent may help identify repeating patterns and candidate mechanisms.
The operating system must still judge what truly has leverage.

---

## 31. Versioning Notes

The Lever Architecture Standard should evolve when any of the following occur:

- repeated work reveals missing categories of leverage or bottleneck not captured well by the current structure
- validation outcomes under T15 show that apparent levers were weaker or more conditional than expected
- platform ecosystem work under T17 requires stronger treatment of platform-controlled amplifiers and dampeners
- T19 learning shows that the system overvalued visible signals and undervalued structural chokepoints
- fast-moving ecosystems require stronger integration with T7 temporal rhythms and T16 refresh protocols

Version updates should record what leverage-intelligence weakness prompted the revision.

---

## 32. Final Standard Statement

Every ecosystem has mechanisms that matter more than they first appear. Some create compounding movement. Others quietly block it.

The Lever Architecture exists to identify those mechanisms so that later strategic work can act where force actually exists instead of where motion merely seems visible.

Without T2, the operating system can describe the ecosystem.
With T2, it can see what actually moves it.

---

For practical use, the Lever Architecture may be maintained in:

The format may vary.
The leverage discipline may not.

# T3 — Participant Decision Architecture Standard

## Document Status
**System:** Market-Intelligence Operating System  
**Layer:** Intelligence Layer  
**Designation:** T3  
**Document Type:** Standard  
**Status:** Canonical Draft  
**Purpose Class:** Decision Process Mapping / Buying Psychology / Commitment Pathway Analysis

---

## 1. Purpose

The **Participant Decision Architecture** is the authoritative decision-process artifact for the Market-Intelligence Operating System.

Its function is to map how each participant type inside an ecosystem evaluates options, processes information, responds to influence, encounters objections, applies shortcuts, and ultimately commits to action or permanent non-decision.

This document exists because knowing that an ecosystem participant has a need is not the same as knowing how that participant decides. A trigger may create urgency. A culture may define belonging. But the actual path from awareness to commitment still runs through a decision process — and that process is often patterned, social, emotionally burdened, and shortcut-driven.

T3 exists to make that decision architecture explicit so downstream sales design, pricing logic, onboarding, content staging, and objection handling can be grounded in how choices are actually made inside the ecosystem rather than how rational decision theory says they should be made.

---

## 2. Strategic Role in the Operating System

The Ecosystem Analysis identifies participants. T2 identifies what makes them act. T3 explains how they translate prompts, pressures, and information into commitment.

T3 serves nine strategic roles:

1. **Decision pathway mapping** — reveals the sequence participants move through from awareness to commitment.
2. **Information design grounding** — identifies what information matters, in what order, and in what format.
3. **Influence interpretation** — distinguishes who meaningfully shifts a participant’s decision and who merely creates noise.
4. **Timeline realism** — clarifies whether decisions are impulsive, considered, committee-based, or habit-driven.
5. **Objection intelligence** — surfaces the recurring barriers that block movement at each stage.
6. **Heuristic exposure** — identifies the shortcuts participants use when they do not fully evaluate options.
7. **Segment differentiation** — prevents a single decision model from being falsely applied across all participant types.
8. **Strategy translation** — feeds downstream pricing, onboarding, content sequencing, positioning, and trust work.
9. **Entry-vector support** — gives T10 a more realistic basis for segment selection and communication logic.

T3 is therefore the operating system’s participant-level decision-intelligence layer.

---

## 3. What This Document Is

This document is:

- a structured map of decision processes by participant type
- a cognitive and social commitment-pathway analysis
- an objection and influence architecture document
- a downstream input to pricing, onboarding, messaging, and content sequencing work
- a participant-specific complement to leverage and culture analysis

---

## 4. What This Document Is Not

The Participant Decision Architecture is **not**:

- a generic buyer journey
- a universal funnel template applied to all ecosystems
- a marketing persona document
- a feature prioritization memo
- a list of preferences without stage logic
- a speculative sales playbook disconnected from evidence

It should not flatten different participant types into one decision model or assume that a visible need automatically implies a predictable buying path.

---

## 5. When to Commission or Use It

T3 should be commissioned once the Ecosystem Analysis has defined distinct participant types and once T2 or other evidence makes it important to understand how those participants convert awareness or urgency into actual commitment.

It becomes mandatory whenever any of the following are true:

- sales, onboarding, pricing, or content-sequencing decisions are approaching
- segment selection depends on whether one participant type is easier or harder to move than another
- T10 Entry Vector Analysis will be produced
- there is material uncertainty about where decisions stall, who influences them, or what objections dominate
- the ecosystem contains multiple participant types with likely different decision logics

T3 is strongest when produced after T2, because trigger knowledge helps clarify when and why decision pathways compress or stall. fileciteturn9file0

---

## 6. Mandatory vs Conditional Use

**Mandatory** for:

- ecosystems with multiple distinct participant types
- opportunities where buying, switching, or trial behavior varies meaningfully by segment
- any effort that will later produce T10 Entry Vector Analysis
- any downstream work involving pricing psychology, onboarding flow design, objection handling, or content-by-stage logic

**Conditionally recommended** for:

- simple ecosystems with only one participant type and a trivial decision process
- early scans where segment-specific commitment pathways are not yet decision-relevant

Where segment-level decision complexity matters, T3 should be treated as required.

---

## 7. Inputs Required

The Participant Decision Architecture requires the following inputs:

### 7.1 Foundational Inputs
- Ecosystem Analysis Section 1 participant map
- Ecosystem Analysis findings on unmet needs, interfaces, and participant interdependence

### 7.2 Intelligence Inputs
- T2 Lever Architecture, especially trigger conditions and urgency windows
- T1 Culture Map where belief systems, status concerns, or social proof mechanics materially shape decisions

### 7.3 Evidence Inputs
- participant-native discussions and self-descriptions
- public decision narratives, reviews, and comparison behavior
- ecosystem-native questions, complaints, and recommendation requests
- community evidence of objections, switching logic, and trust cues

### 7.4 Governance Inputs
- evidence standards from the Ecosystem Analysis Standard
- explicit assumption flagging where participant decision logic is partly inferential

---

## 8. Outputs Produced

T3 produces the following outputs:

- one complete decision architecture for each participant type
- ranked information sources and format preferences
- influence chains and anti-influences
- decision-speed baselines and compression / extension factors
- objection catalogues organized by stage
- dominant heuristics used when evaluation is incomplete

These outputs feed directly into T6, T10, and later pricing, onboarding, content, and trust design work. fileciteturn9file0turn8file7

---

## 9. Position in Sequencing Logic

T3 belongs in the Intelligence layer after participant types are known and ideally after T2 has mapped triggers.

Recommended sequence:

1. Ecosystem Analysis completed
2. T2 Lever Architecture produced or underway
3. **T3 Participant Decision Architecture produced**
4. T6 Trust Architecture may later use T3 to understand who matters in the trust chain
5. T10 Entry Vector Analysis may later use T3 for segment selection and vector formation

T3 can be produced standalone, but it is strongest when T2 already exists because trigger knowledge informs decision-timeline mapping. fileciteturn9file0

---

## 10. Relationship to Other Documents

### Upstream Relationships
T3 depends primarily on:

- **Ecosystem Analysis §1** for the participant map
- **T2 Lever Architecture** for trigger knowledge and urgency logic
- **T1 Culture Map** where identity, taboo, and status visibly influence how participants decide

### Downstream Relationships
T3 directly supports:

- **T6 Trust Architecture** by clarifying who influences decisions and when trust matters most
- **T10 Entry Vector Analysis** by defining which participant type and decision path a vector serves
- later sales design, pricing psychology, onboarding architecture, content sequencing, and objection-handling work after the analytical stack is complete

---

## 11. Core Analytical Questions

T3 should allow a reviewer to answer the following questions quickly:

1. Where does each participant type get decision-relevant information?
2. In what sequence do they evaluate options?
3. Who actually influences their choice?
4. What kind of social proof matters most?
5. How quickly do they decide, and what compresses or extends that timeline?
6. What objections appear at each stage?
7. Which objections are rational versus emotional?
8. What heuristics dominate when they cannot or will not evaluate fully?
9. What signals cause abandonment or permanent non-decision?
10. How do decision patterns differ meaningfully across participant types?

---

## 12. Required Structure of the Document

One complete Decision Architecture must be produced for **each participant type** identified in the Ecosystem Analysis. If the ecosystem has four participant types, the document contains four parallel analyses. fileciteturn8file3

Each participant analysis must contain the following five sections.

### 12.1 Section 1 — Information Architecture
**Governing question:** Where does this participant get the information that informs their decisions?

This section must document:

- primary information sources ranked by trust and frequency of use
- information-format preferences
- information sequence, such as what is checked first, second, and third
- information vetoes, meaning pieces of information that kill the decision immediately

The goal is to map not just sources, but the participant’s order of operations. fileciteturn8file7

### 12.2 Section 2 — Influence Chain
**Governing question:** Who influences this participant’s decisions, and in what sequence?

This section must document:

- primary influencers the participant consults or observes
- authority weight for each influencer type
- social-proof mechanics that are most persuasive
- anti-influences, meaning endorsements or associations that actually damage adoption

This section should distinguish visible attention from actual decision-shifting authority. fileciteturn8file7

### 12.3 Section 3 — Decision Timeline
**Governing question:** How long does this participant take to decide, and what can compress or extend that timeline?

This section must document:

- baseline decision speed
- compression factors, including those linked to T2 triggers
- extension factors that introduce delay
- abandonment threshold, meaning when delay becomes permanent non-decision

This section should reveal whether time is a soft variable or a hard strategic constraint. fileciteturn8file7

### 12.4 Section 4 — Objection Catalogue
**Governing question:** What objections arise at each decision stage, and what resolves them?

For each participant type, objections must be organized by decision stage:

- awareness-stage objections
- consideration-stage objections
- decision-stage objections
- post-decision objections

For each objection, document:

- the objection in the participant’s own language
- what evidence or experience resolves it
- whether the objection is rational or emotional

The catalogue must distinguish information gaps from trust or identity barriers. fileciteturn8file7

### 12.5 Section 5 — The Decision Heuristic
**Governing question:** When this participant doesn’t have time or energy for full evaluation, what shortcut do they use?

This section must identify the dominant heuristics used by the participant, such as:

- authority heuristic
- social proof heuristic
- price-as-quality heuristic
- default heuristic
- risk-aversion heuristic

The section must identify which heuristic dominates for each participant type and why. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 13. Required Data Fields

At minimum, every T3 document should include the following fields.

### 13.1 Header Fields
- **Document ID**
- **Ecosystem name**
- **Version**
- **Date**
- **Prepared by**
- **Source Ecosystem Analysis ID**
- **Status**

### 13.2 Participant Fields
- **Participant type**
- **Role in ecosystem**
- **Primary unmet need**
- **Relevant trigger references**

### 13.3 Information Architecture Fields
- **Source**
- **Trust rank**
- **Usage frequency**
- **Preferred format**
- **Sequence position**
- **Veto condition**

### 13.4 Influence Chain Fields
- **Influencer type**
- **Authority weight**
- **Influence sequence position**
- **Persuasive social-proof type**
- **Anti-influence note**

### 13.5 Decision Timeline Fields
- **Baseline speed**
- **Compression factor**
- **Extension factor**
- **Abandonment threshold**

### 13.6 Objection Fields
- **Decision stage**
- **Objection statement**
- **Resolution path**
- **Rational / emotional classification**

### 13.7 Heuristic Fields
- **Heuristic type**
- **Dominance level**
- **Why it dominates**
- **Implication for downstream strategy**

---

## 14. Information Architecture Standard

This section must reveal the participant’s information stack, not just a list of sources.

It should identify:

- which sources the participant trusts most
- which sources they actually use most often
- how format preference changes the credibility of a message
- what sequence they follow during real evaluation
- what immediate vetoes end evaluation regardless of other positives

The distinction between trusted information and consumed information should be preserved where they differ.

---

## 15. Influence Chain Standard

This section must map the social and informational chain around the participant’s decision.

It should identify:

- who the participant consults directly
- who they observe without consulting
- which endorsements meaningfully shift the outcome
- which associations create backlash or mistrust

Authority weight should be ranked, not merely described. The point is to show which influences actually move the participant. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 16. Decision Timeline Standard

The timeline section must distinguish between the participant’s default pace and the conditions that distort it.

Recommended baseline categories:

- impulse (minutes)
- considered (days to weeks)
- enterprise (weeks to months)

Compression factors should explicitly reference T2 where urgency triggers exist. Extension factors should include internal approval, budget timing, evaluation burden, switching cost, or social-risk concerns where relevant. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 17. Objection Catalogue Standard

The objection catalogue is one of the most operationally valuable parts of T3.

It must:

- organize objections by stage rather than lumping them together
- state objections in participant-native language where possible
- identify what resolves each objection
- distinguish rational objections from emotional objections

Rational objections are usually resolved with information, proof, compatibility, or economics.
Emotional objections are usually resolved with trust, social proof, status safety, or lived reassurance. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 18. Decision Heuristic Standard

This section must identify the shortcut logic participants use under cognitive or time pressure.

Most participants do not evaluate fully every time. T3 must therefore document which heuristic dominates when decision energy is low.

Recommended heuristic families include:

- authority
- social proof
- price-as-quality
- default / platform recommendation
- risk-aversion

Where possible, the analysis should explain when the participant shifts from full evaluation to heuristic use. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 19. Research Methodology Standard

The Participant Decision Architecture should follow a participant-level decision-mapping methodology.

Recommended minimum protocol:

### 19.1 Decision Evidence Scan
Collect participant-native evidence showing how options are compared, discussed, accepted, or rejected.

### 19.2 Recommendation Behavior Review
Analyze recommendation requests, peer advice patterns, reviews, and self-reported switching logic.

### 19.3 Objection Mining
Read high-friction threads, support complaints, review negatives, and hesitation language to identify stage-specific objections.

### 19.4 Heuristic Detection
Look for repeated shortcuts in phrasing, such as deference to authority, price shorthand, default choices, or visible crowd-following.

The methodology should prioritize actual decision behavior over idealized rational models.

---

## 20. Source and Evidence Expectations

The Participant Decision Architecture must remain evidence-based even when parts of the journey are partly inferred.

Source expectations:

- use participant-native material where possible
- distinguish observed decision patterns from inferred ones
- preserve participant phrasing for objections where analytically useful
- use repeated patterns before declaring a dominant heuristic or influence chain when feasible
- flag insufficient data explicitly rather than masking it with generic assumptions

Primary community and participant expression should be preferred over abstract marketing theory. fileciteturn9file0

---

## 21. Length Calibration

Recommended target length: **1,200–1,800 words per participant type**.  
For a four-participant ecosystem, the full document target is **4,800–7,200 words**.  
Maximum: **10,000 words total**. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 22. Required Usage Rules

The following rules are mandatory.

### Rule 1 — One architecture per participant type
Each participant type identified in the Ecosystem Analysis must receive a complete five-section analysis. fileciteturn8file3

### Rule 2 — No generic buyer-journey substitution
T3 must reflect ecosystem-specific decision logic rather than imported funnel assumptions.

### Rule 3 — No trigger blindness
Decision-timeline analysis must incorporate T2 compression and urgency logic where relevant. fileciteturn9file0

### Rule 4 — No objection flattening
Objections must be staged and classified, not collapsed into a single list.

### Rule 5 — No rationality overstatement
Heuristics must be treated as real decision drivers where evidence suggests shortcut behavior.

### Rule 6 — No product contamination
T3 may not contain product names, feature lists, or build plans. fileciteturn9file0

### Rule 7 — No hidden data gaps
If a section lacks sufficient evidence, it must be flagged explicitly and may recommend interviews, surveys, or community observation to fill the gap. fileciteturn8file7

---

## 23. Recommended Operating Procedure

### Step 1 — Anchor in the participant map
Use the Ecosystem Analysis to define the participant types requiring analysis.

### Step 2 — Import trigger context from T2
Clarify what conditions cause each participant to enter active decision mode.

### Step 3 — Map information and influence structure
Identify who informs the decision, in what order, and with what authority.

### Step 4 — Map timeline and abandonment dynamics
Clarify how quickly the participant decides and what lengthens or compresses the process.

### Step 5 — Catalogue objections by stage
Capture recurring objections in participant-native language and identify what resolves them.

### Step 6 — Identify dominant heuristics
Determine what shortcut logic takes over when the participant does not fully evaluate.

### Step 7 — Repeat for each participant type
Produce a complete architecture for every participant type in the ecosystem.

---

## 24. Quality Standard / Acceptance Criteria

The Participant Decision Architecture should be considered acceptable only if all of the following are true:

1. Every participant type has a complete five-section analysis.
2. Information sources are ranked and sequenced, not merely listed.
3. Influence chains identify real decision-shifting actors and anti-influences.
4. Decision timelines distinguish baseline speed, compression factors, extension factors, and abandonment threshold.
5. Objections are organized by stage and classified as rational or emotional.
6. Dominant heuristics are identified clearly.
7. T2 trigger logic is reflected where relevant.
8. Evidence and inference are distinguished where needed.
9. Data gaps are explicitly flagged when present.
10. The document gives downstream work a realistic picture of how each participant type commits to action.

---

## 25. Failure Modes and Misuse Warnings

Common failure modes include:

### 25.1 Generic funnel drift
Using standard buyer-journey language without ecosystem-specific evidence.

### 25.2 Segment collapse
Treating all participant types as though they decide in the same way.

### 25.3 Trigger omission
Ignoring the urgency or switching conditions already identified in T2.

### 25.4 Objection vagueness
Stating generic concerns like “trust” or “price” without stage, language, or resolution path.

### 25.5 Heuristic blindness
Assuming participants evaluate rationally when they clearly use shortcuts.

### 25.6 Social-proof simplification
Treating all social proof as interchangeable rather than source- and context-dependent.

### 25.7 Evidence thinness
Making strong claims about participant psychology from weak or one-off observations.

---

## 26. Minimum Template

The following structure should be included in implementation.

## 26.1 Participant Decision Architecture Template

### Header
- Document ID
- Ecosystem Name
- Version
- Date
- Prepared By
- Source Ecosystem Analysis ID
- Status

### Participant Block
- Participant Type
- Role in Ecosystem
- Primary Unmet Need
- Relevant Trigger References

### 1. Information Architecture
| Source | Trust Rank | Frequency of Use | Preferred Format | Sequence Position | Veto Condition |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|

### 2. Influence Chain
| Influencer Type | Authority Weight | Sequence Position | Most Persuasive Social Proof | Anti-Influence Note |
|---|---|---|---|---|

### 3. Decision Timeline
| Baseline Speed | Compression Factors | Extension Factors | Abandonment Threshold |
|---|---|---|---|

### 4. Objection Catalogue
| Decision Stage | Objection Statement | Resolution Path | Rational / Emotional |
|---|---|---|---|

### 5. Decision Heuristic
| Heuristic Type | Dominance Level | Why It Dominates | Strategic Implication |
|---|---|---|---|

### 6. Supporting References
- Ecosystem Analysis
- T2
- T1 if relevant
- participant-native discussions, reviews, and observed decision evidence

---

## 27. Relationship to Agentic Research Systems

The Participant Decision Architecture should be structured so that agents can assist with gathering participant-native decision evidence without mistaking extraction for interpretation.

This means:

- each participant type should have a stable block structure
- information, influence, timeline, objections, and heuristics should be separated cleanly
- participant-native language should be preserved where useful
- inferred patterns should remain distinguishable from directly observed ones
- explicit data gaps should be recordable rather than hidden

An agent may help surface decision patterns and stage-specific objections.
The operating system must still judge what the participant’s decision logic actually is.

---

## 28. Versioning Notes

The Participant Decision Architecture Standard should evolve when any of the following occur:

- repeated work reveals missing decision-pattern categories or heuristic families
- downstream validation under T15 shows that objections were poorly staged or incorrectly classified
- trust analysis under T6 exposes missing influence-chain detail
- T19 learning shows that segment selection failed because the wrong participant decision model was assumed
- platform ecosystems under T17 require stronger treatment of platform-mediated default heuristics or review-based vetoes

Version updates should record what decision-intelligence weakness prompted the revision.

---

## 29. Final Standard Statement

A participant does not move from need to commitment in a straight line. They move through information, influence, hesitation, shortcuts, and social risk.

The Participant Decision Architecture exists to make that path visible for each participant type in the ecosystem. It is the difference between knowing that someone should act and understanding how they actually decide.

Without T3, the operating system can identify who matters and what pressures exist.
With T3, it can understand how those people actually choose.

---

- impulse
- considered

### Objection Stage Enumeration
- awareness

- post-decision

### Objection Classification Enumeration
- rational
- emotional
- mixed

### Heuristic Type Enumeration
- authority
- social proof

- default
- risk-aversion
- convenience / familiarity

### Status Enumeration
- draft
- under review
- intelligence-ready
- refreshed
- archived

---

## Appendix B — Implementation Guidance

For practical use, the Participant Decision Architecture may be maintained in:

- structured markdown
- intelligence research repositories
- participant-level decision databases with stable IDs
- evidence-backed narrative documents organized by participant block

The format may vary.
The decision-mapping discipline may not.

# T4 — Language & Semiotics Map Standard

## Document Status
**System:** Market-Intelligence Operating System  
**Layer:** Intelligence Layer  
**Designation:** T4  
**Document Type:** Standard  
**Status:** Canonical Draft  
**Purpose Class:** Communication Intelligence / Lexicon Mapping / Symbolic Fit Analysis

---

## 1. Purpose

The **Language & Semiotics Map** is the authoritative communication-intelligence artifact for the Market-Intelligence Operating System.

Its function is to document the lexicon, metaphors, emotional register, symbolic vocabulary, platform-specific language shifts, and forbidden expressions that govern how an ecosystem speaks and how it recognizes insider versus outsider communication.

This document exists because the Culture Map explains what the ecosystem believes, but not how those beliefs are actually expressed in words, tone, and symbols. T4 is the operational translation layer that converts cultural understanding into usable communication intelligence.

Every ecosystem speaks its own dialect. Using the wrong word, the wrong metaphor, or the wrong tone can signal outsider status even when the underlying concept is correct. T4 exists to prevent that mismatch and to make the ecosystem’s communication patterns explicit. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 2. Strategic Role in the Operating System

The Ecosystem Analysis defines the ecosystem. T1 reveals the culture. T4 explains how that culture sounds in practice.

T4 serves nine strategic roles:

1. **Lexical grounding** — identifies the terms that carry real cultural weight.
2. **Meaning clarification** — distinguishes literal meaning from cultural meaning.
3. **Register control** — maps the tonal spectrum of the ecosystem.
4. **Metaphor detection** — identifies the recurring cognitive frames that shape communication.
5. **Platform adaptation** — shows how the same community modulates language across different surfaces.
6. **Taboo protection** — identifies words and tonal patterns that trigger distrust or rejection.
7. **Naming intelligence** — clarifies what naming patterns feel native versus foreign.
8. **Copywriting readiness** — feeds downstream UI copy, listings, support scripts, positioning language, and brand naming.
9. **Cultural authenticity support** — ensures that communication artifacts sound like they belong inside the ecosystem rather than hovering above it.

T4 is therefore the operating system’s communication-intelligence layer. fileciteturn10file0turn10file2

---

## 3. What This Document Is

This document is:

- a structured map of ecosystem language and symbolic vocabulary
- a lexicon and metaphor analysis
- a tonal and register map
- a platform-specific communication-variation document
- a downstream input to copywriting, UI text, support scripts, naming, and positioning

---

## 4. What This Document Is Not

The Language & Semiotics Map is **not**:

- a brand voice guide for a specific product
- a copy deck
- a list of trendy words without cultural context
- a general dictionary
- a messaging framework detached from ecosystem evidence
- a naming brainstorm

It should not replace the Culture Map, and it should not drift into product-specific communication artifacts.

---

## 5. When to Commission or Use It

T4 should be commissioned when an ecosystem has been defined and there is a need to understand how its beliefs, values, and identity are actually expressed in language, symbols, tone, and platform-native phrasing.

It becomes mandatory whenever any of the following are true:

- copywriting, UI microcopy, listing descriptions, support scripts, or brand naming are imminent
- trust and credibility depend on sounding native rather than merely accurate
- messaging, content, or support language must be ecosystem-specific
- T1 Culture Map already suggests strong insider-outsider distinctions in how language is used

T4 is strongest when produced after T1, because cultural context deepens language interpretation. It can be produced standalone, but it loses depth when the cultural layer is absent. fileciteturn10file0turn10file1turn11file0

---

## 6. Mandatory vs Conditional Use

**Mandatory** for:

- any effort that will later create copy, naming, support language, or positioning language
- ecosystems where outsider wording is likely to trigger distrust or cringe
- any case where T1 has identified strong rituals, sub-tribes, or taboo boundaries that clearly affect speech patterns

**Conditionally recommended** for:

- highly technical or infrastructure ecosystems where language fit matters less, but still affects trust and clarity
- early ecosystem scans where communication work is not yet imminent

Where language fit materially affects adoption, T4 should be treated as required.

---

## 7. Inputs Required

The Language & Semiotics Map requires the following inputs:

### 7.1 Foundational Inputs
- Ecosystem Analysis Section 1 participant map
- T1 Culture Map, especially creed, icons, rituals, sacred/profane boundaries, and sub-tribes

### 7.2 Evidence Inputs
- primary community content
- comments, threads, and forum language
- creator scripts, captions, and titles
- platform-native abbreviations, emoji conventions, and in-group phrasing
- ecosystem-native naming patterns for tools, roles, formats, and practices

### 7.3 Governance Inputs
- source quality standards from the Ecosystem Analysis Standard
- explicit assumption flagging when symbolic meaning is inferential rather than directly observable
- the Intelligence Suite requirement that language observations use actual community vocabulary, not analyst paraphrases fileciteturn10file9turn11file0

---

## 8. Outputs Produced

T4 produces the following outputs:

- a core lexicon of ecosystem-specific terms
- a metaphor system map
- an emotional and tonal register map
- platform-specific language variations
- a forbidden lexicon of words and tonal patterns that trigger distrust or rejection
- naming conventions for products, features, roles, and formats

These outputs feed directly into copywriting, UI microcopy, marketing communications, listing descriptions, customer support scripts, and brand naming. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 9. Position in Sequencing Logic

T4 belongs in the Intelligence layer and is strongest after T1.

Recommended sequence:

1. Ecosystem Analysis completed
2. T1 Culture Map produced
3. **T4 Language & Semiotics Map produced**
4. T6 Trust Architecture may later use taboo and trust-language dynamics
5. T10 Entry Vector Analysis may later use T4 for language fit within vector design
6. downstream communication work begins only after the analytical stack has been completed sufficiently

T4 can be produced before T3 or after T3. Its primary dependency is cultural context, not decision-pathway sequencing. fileciteturn10file0turn10file1

---

## 10. Relationship to Other Documents

### Upstream Relationships
T4 depends primarily on:

- **Ecosystem Analysis §1** for participant and ecosystem context
- **T1 Culture Map** for cultural beliefs, sub-tribes, status structure, and taboo boundaries

### Downstream Relationships
T4 directly supports:

- **T6 Trust Architecture** by clarifying language patterns that increase or destroy credibility
- **T10 Entry Vector Analysis** by supplying language-fit inputs to vector communication logic
- all later copywriting, UI copy, support scripting, marketing language, listing descriptions, and naming work after the analysis phase is complete

---

## 11. Core Analytical Questions

T4 should allow a reviewer to answer the following questions quickly:

1. What words and phrases matter most in this ecosystem?
2. What do those words mean literally, and what do they mean culturally?
3. What metaphor systems shape how participants think and talk?
4. What emotional tone dominates the ecosystem?
5. How does that tone change across platforms and contexts?
6. What words or tonal patterns trigger distrust, cringe, or hostility?
7. What naming patterns feel native in this ecosystem?
8. Which words are outdated, overused, appropriated, or culturally unsafe?
9. How do sub-tribes or platform surfaces alter language use?
10. What would make communication feel like it was written by someone who belongs? fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 12. Required Structure of the Document

Every Language & Semiotics Map must contain the following six sections. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

### 12.1 Section 1 — The Core Lexicon
**Governing question:** What are the 30–60 most important words and phrases in this ecosystem, and what do they actually mean?

This section must build a structured glossary of ecosystem-specific terminology.

For each term, document:

- the term itself exactly as used, including capitalization, abbreviation, slang, or emoji convention where relevant
- literal meaning
- cultural meaning
- usage context
- caution notes about misuse or outsider signaling

This is not a general dictionary. It is a map of words that carry cultural weight. The lexicon must use actual community vocabulary rather than analyst paraphrases. fileciteturn10file2turn10file9turn11file0

### 12.2 Section 2 — The Metaphor System
**Governing question:** What recurring metaphors structure how participants think and talk about their experience?

This section must identify 3–5 dominant metaphor families in the ecosystem.

For each metaphor family, document:

- source domain
- what concepts it illuminates
- what it obscures or de-emphasizes
- whether it is shared ecosystem-wide or contested across sub-tribes
- whether it is alive, weakening, or dead through overuse

Metaphors are not decorative. They are cognitive frameworks that shape what kinds of messages resonate and what kinds feel wrong. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

### 12.3 Section 3 — Emotional Register
**Governing question:** What is the dominant emotional tone, and how does it vary across contexts?

This section must map the tonal spectrum across the following dimensions:

- formality: casual to professional
- certainty: tentative to assertive
- emotion: analytical to passionate
- accessibility: specialist to populist
- humour: serious to irreverent

For each dimension, document:

- where the ecosystem sits overall
- when the register shifts
- what topics or surfaces cause tonal variation

This section should show not only the dominant tone, but also the contexts in which tone changes. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

### 12.4 Section 4 — Platform-Specific Variations
**Governing question:** How does the same community modulate its language across different platforms?

This section must document platform-specific language shifts for each major platform the ecosystem uses.

For each platform, document:

- format constraints
- platform-native vocabulary or abbreviations
- tonal shifts relative to the ecosystem baseline
- differences in supportiveness, competitiveness, professionalism, or irreverence

The same community often speaks differently on TikTok, LinkedIn, Reddit, Discord, YouTube, X, forums, or email. T4 must preserve those differences rather than flattening them. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

### 12.5 Section 5 — The Forbidden Lexicon
**Governing question:** What words, phrases, and tonal patterns actively trigger distrust, cringe, or hostility?

This section must identify language patterns that function as negative signals.

Required categories include:

- corporate-speak that signals inauthenticity
- outdated terminology that signals ignorance
- appropriated language that feels extractive
- toxic-positivity markers that dismiss legitimate concerns
- overpromise language that triggers skepticism based on prior bad actors

This section is as important as the core lexicon, because the fastest way to be rejected by a community is often to use the wrong words. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

### 12.6 Section 6 — Naming Conventions
**Governing question:** What patterns govern how things are named within the ecosystem, and what naming choices signal belonging?

This section must document naming patterns for:

- products and tools
- features or capabilities
- content formats
- community roles and participant labels

For each pattern, clarify whether the ecosystem tends to prefer:

- technical names
- playful names
- metaphorical names
- functional names

This section should also distinguish patterns associated with successful versus poorly received naming choices inside the space. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 13. Required Data Fields

At minimum, every T4 document should include the following fields.

### 13.1 Header Fields
- **Document ID**
- **Ecosystem name**
- **Version**
- **Date**
- **Prepared by**
- **Source Ecosystem Analysis ID**
- **Status**

### 13.2 Core Lexicon Fields
- **Term**
- **Literal meaning**
- **Cultural meaning**
- **Usage context**
- **Caution note**

### 13.3 Metaphor Fields
- **Metaphor family**
- **Source domain**
- **What it illuminates**
- **What it obscures**
- **Sub-tribe variation note**
- **Alive / dead / fading status**

### 13.4 Register Fields
- **Dimension**
- **Spectrum**
- **Where the ecosystem sits**
- **Contextual shift note**

### 13.5 Platform Variation Fields
- **Platform**
- **Format constraint**
- **Platform-native vocabulary**
- **Tonal shift**
- **Implication for communication**

### 13.6 Forbidden Lexicon Fields
- **Word / phrase / tonal pattern**
- **Why it triggers distrust**
- **Type of violation**
- **Context where it is most dangerous**

### 13.7 Naming Convention Fields
- **Naming domain**
- **Observed pattern**
- **What it signals**
- **Successful / weak pattern note**

---

## 14. Core Lexicon Standard

The core lexicon is the anchor of T4.

It must:

- include a minimum of 30 terms
- use terms exactly as the community uses them
- distinguish literal meaning from cultural meaning for every term
- show where the term appears and what misuse would signal

A term should only appear if it carries practical or symbolic weight inside the ecosystem. Generic language does not qualify. The Intelligence Suite quality checklist explicitly requires T4’s core lexicon to distinguish literal from cultural meaning for every term. fileciteturn10file9turn11file0

---

## 15. Metaphor System Standard

This section must treat metaphors as operating frames rather than rhetorical flourishes.

A strong metaphor analysis should show:

- which domains the ecosystem borrows from repeatedly
- which metaphors feel central versus peripheral
- whether different sub-tribes use different metaphor families
- which metaphor systems have gone stale and now signal cliché rather than insight

The aim is to understand not just how the ecosystem speaks, but how it organizes thought through language. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 16. Emotional Register Standard

The emotional register section must preserve tonal complexity.

It should clarify:

- the ecosystem’s default tone
- how formal or informal it tends to be
- when hedging is preferred versus when certainty is rewarded
- where passion is acceptable and where cool analysis is expected
- when humor is allowed and when it becomes taboo

Register should be documented with context, not as a single static label. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 17. Platform-Specific Variations Standard

This section must analyze modulation, not mere channel presence.

For each major platform, clarify:

- what the format encourages or suppresses
- how language compresses or expands
- which abbreviations or symbolic conventions are native there
- how tone changes relative to the same speaker elsewhere

The point is to map how the ecosystem changes voice across surfaces without losing identity. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 18. Forbidden Lexicon Standard

This section must identify language patterns that produce immediate credibility damage.

The forbidden lexicon should capture at least 10 entries and should prioritize:

- phrases associated with outsiders, marketers, or scammers
- terms that were once acceptable but now mark ignorance
- language borrowed from the culture in ways that feel inauthentic or extractive
- tonal patterns that feel dismissive, overpromising, or culturally false

This section should be sourced from community response patterns rather than analyst dislike. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 19. Naming Conventions Standard

This section must identify how naming works inside the ecosystem.

It should clarify:

- whether successful names are technical, playful, metaphorical, or functional
- how features tend to be named
- how roles and formats are labeled
- what naming styles feel native versus contrived

The purpose is not to invent names. The purpose is to understand what naming patterns the ecosystem already trusts. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 20. Research Methodology Standard

The Language & Semiotics Map should follow a language-immersion and pattern-capture methodology.

Recommended minimum protocol:

### 20.1 Lexicon Harvest
Collect recurring ecosystem terms, abbreviations, emoji conventions, and repeated phrases from primary community content.

### 20.2 Metaphor Detection
Read creator content, comments, threads, and longer-form explanations to identify recurring source domains and explanatory frames.

### 20.3 Tone Mapping
Compare how the ecosystem sounds in different contexts: peer conversation, sponsored content, support language, commentary, criticism, and celebration.

### 20.4 Negative Signal Review
Identify moments where communities reject language, mock a phrase, call something cringe, or accuse an actor of sounding fake.

### 20.5 Naming Pattern Audit
Review names of tools, features, content formats, and participant labels that have gained traction or been dismissed.

The methodology should prioritize actual ecosystem wording over cleaned-up interpretation. fileciteturn10file9turn11file0

---

## 21. Source and Evidence Expectations

The Language & Semiotics Map must remain grounded in real ecosystem speech.

Source expectations:

- primary community content should constitute at least 40% of sourced observations in T4 fileciteturn10file9turn11file0
- language observations should use actual community vocabulary, not analyst paraphrases fileciteturn10file9turn11file0
- outsider descriptions should not dominate symbolic interpretation when native speech is available
- inferred symbolic meaning should be marked as interpretive when direct evidence is thin

T4 should be evidence-based even when the analysis is semiotic.

---

## 22. Length Calibration

Recommended target length: **3,000–4,500 words**, excluding the lexicon table.  
Maximum: **6,500 words**. fileciteturn10file2turn11file0

---

## 23. Required Usage Rules

The following rules are mandatory.

### Rule 1 — No analyst paraphrase substitution
Actual community vocabulary must anchor the lexicon and taboo analysis. fileciteturn10file9turn11file0

### Rule 2 — No literal-meaning-only glossaries
Every core lexicon entry must distinguish literal from cultural meaning. fileciteturn10file9turn11file0

### Rule 3 — No product contamination
T4 may not contain product names, feature lists, pricing recommendations, or build plans. fileciteturn10file0turn10file9

### Rule 4 — No platform flattening
Language shifts across platforms must be captured where the ecosystem uses multiple surfaces.

### Rule 5 — No taboo omission
The forbidden lexicon must be treated as strategically important, not optional.

### Rule 6 — No naming speculation detached from ecosystem evidence
Naming conventions must be derived from observed patterns inside the ecosystem.

### Rule 7 — No generic language advice
Every observation must be ecosystem-specific rather than broadly applicable to any market. fileciteturn10file9

---

## 24. Recommended Operating Procedure

### Step 1 — Anchor in the Culture Map
Use T1 to understand the underlying creed, taboo boundaries, and sub-tribe structure.

### Step 2 — Harvest the live language
Collect real terms, phrases, metaphors, and tonal patterns from primary community content.

### Step 3 — Build the core lexicon
Separate literal meaning from cultural meaning and preserve usage context.

### Step 4 — Map metaphor and register systems
Identify the cognitive frames and tonal spectrum that structure communication.

### Step 5 — Compare platform-specific speech patterns
Document how the same ecosystem modulates its language across different surfaces.

### Step 6 — Build the forbidden lexicon and naming pattern map
Capture the words, tonal patterns, and naming conventions that govern trust and belonging.

### Step 7 — Produce the six-section document
Translate the evidence into a communication-intelligence artifact that downstream work can use.

---

## 25. Quality Standard / Acceptance Criteria

The Language & Semiotics Map should be considered acceptable only if all of the following are true:

1. The core lexicon contains at least 30 terms.
2. Every core lexicon entry distinguishes literal meaning from cultural meaning.
3. The metaphor system identifies 3–5 dominant metaphor families.
4. The emotional register maps the ecosystem across the defined tonal dimensions.
5. Platform-specific language variations are documented where relevant.
6. The forbidden lexicon contains at least 10 entries.
7. Naming conventions are derived from observed ecosystem patterns.
8. Primary community content materially anchors the document.
9. The document remains product-agnostic.
10. The final output gives downstream communication work a clear map of how the ecosystem actually sounds. fileciteturn10file2turn10file9turn11file0

---

## 26. Failure Modes and Misuse Warnings

Common failure modes include:

### 26.1 Trendword collection
Listing fashionable phrases without explaining what they mean culturally.

### 26.2 Analyst translation drift
Replacing native language with sanitized analyst wording.

### 26.3 Metaphor underanalysis
Treating metaphors as decorative rather than as conceptual frames.

### 26.4 Tone flattening
Assigning a single tone to a community that actually shifts sharply by context or platform.

### 26.5 Forbidden-language blindness
Ignoring the words and tonal patterns most likely to trigger rejection.

### 26.6 Naming fantasy
Using the document to invent names rather than map naming patterns.

### 26.7 Evidence thinness
Asserting symbolic meaning without enough direct language evidence.

---

## 27. Minimum Template

The following structure should be included in implementation.

## 27.1 Language & Semiotics Map Template

### Header
- Document ID
- Ecosystem Name
- Version
- Date
- Prepared By
- Source Ecosystem Analysis ID
- Status

### 1. The Core Lexicon
| Term | Literal Meaning | Cultural Meaning | Usage Context | Caution Note |
|---|---|---|---|---|

### 2. The Metaphor System
| Metaphor Family | Source Domain | What It Illuminates | What It Obscures | Sub-Tribe Variation | Status |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|

### 3. Emotional Register
| Dimension | Spectrum | Where the Ecosystem Sits | Contextual Shifts |
|---|---|---|---|

### 4. Platform-Specific Variations
| Platform | Format Constraint | Platform-Native Vocabulary | Tonal Shift | Implication for Communication |
|---|---|---|---|---|

### 5. The Forbidden Lexicon
| Word / Phrase / Pattern | Why It Triggers Distrust | Type of Violation | Dangerous Context |
|---|---|---|---|

### 6. Naming Conventions
| Naming Domain | Observed Pattern | What It Signals | Successful / Weak Pattern Note |
|---|---|---|---|

### 7. Supporting References
- Ecosystem Analysis
- T1 Culture Map
- primary community content
- creator content
- threads, comments, and platform-native language sources

---

## 28. Relationship to Agentic Research Systems

The Language & Semiotics Map should be structured so that agents can help collect vocabulary, phrase clusters, and platform-specific patterns without collapsing interpretation into keyword frequency alone.

This means:

- lexicon entries should be discrete and stable
- literal and cultural meaning should be separated clearly
- platform-specific variations should be recorded independently
- forbidden-language observations should remain linked to actual usage evidence
- interpretive conclusions should remain reviewable rather than fully automated

An agent may help gather terms and pattern clusters.
The operating system must still judge what those terms mean inside the culture.

---

## 29. Versioning Notes

The Language & Semiotics Map Standard should evolve when any of the following occur:

- repeated work shows that key symbolic domains are being missed
- T6 trust work reveals missing taboo or negative-language patterns
- T10 synthesis reveals that language-fit analysis needs stronger integration with vector scoring
- T16 refresh cycles show that ecosystem language evolves faster than the current method captures
- T19 learning shows that communication failed despite strong cultural analysis because semiotic fit was misunderstood

Version updates should record what communication-intelligence weakness prompted the revision.

---

## 30. Final Standard Statement

A culture is not fully understood until its language is understood.

The Language & Semiotics Map exists to document how an ecosystem’s beliefs, boundaries, and identity become words, metaphors, tone, and naming patterns. It is the layer that makes cultural fit operational for communication.

Without T4, the operating system can understand what the ecosystem believes.
With T4, it can understand how the ecosystem speaks.

---

- formality
- certainty
- emotion
- accessibility
- humour

### Platform Variation Enumeration
- formalization shift
- slang compression
- abbreviation shift
- humor shift
- supportiveness shift
- competitiveness shift

### Forbidden Lexicon Type Enumeration
- corporate-speak
- outdated terminology
- appropriated language
- toxic positivity
- overpromise language
- scam-adjacent phrasing

### Naming Pattern Enumeration
- technical
- playful
- metaphorical
- functional
- hybrid

### Status Enumeration

- under review
- intelligence-ready
- refreshed
- archived

---

## Appendix B — Implementation Guidance

For practical use, the Language & Semiotics Map may be maintained in:

- structured markdown
- intelligence research repositories
- lexicon databases with stable IDs
- evidence-backed narrative documents with linked tables for terms and symbolic patterns

The format may vary.
The communication-intelligence discipline may not.

FILE 17/22: t_5_competitive_topology_standard.md
